10041-8{_}1314{_}He forgave; when Patricia wept, seeing how leaden-colored his handsome face had turned, he forgave as promptly and as freely as he was learning to pardon the telling of a serviceable lie, or the perpetration of an occasional barbarism in speech, by Patricia.
10041-8{_}1315{_}For he, a Musgrave of Matocton, had married a Stapylton; he had begun to comprehend that their standards were different, and that some daily conflict between these standards was inevitable.
10041-8{_}1316{_}And besides, as it has been veraciously observed, the truth of an insult is the barb which prevents its retraction.
10041-8{_}1317{_}Patricia spoke the truth: Rudolph Musgrave and all those rationally reliant upon Rudolph Musgrave for support, had lived for some five years upon the money which they owed to Patricia.
10041-8{_}1318{_}He saw about him other scions of old families who accepted such circumstances blithely: but, he said, he was a Musgrave of Matocton; and, he reflected, in the kingdom of the blind the one-eyed is necessarily very unhappy.
10041-8{_}1319{_}He did not mean to touch a penny of such moneys as Roger Stapylton had bequeathed to him; for the colonel considered--now--it was a man's duty personally to support his wife and child and sister.
10041-8{_}1320{_}And he vigorously attempted to discharge this obligation, alike by virtue of his salary at the Library, and by spasmodic raids upon his tiny capital, and--chief of all--by speculation in the Stock Market.
10041-8{_}1321{_}Oddly enough, his ventures were through a long while--for the most part--successful.
10041-8{_}1322{_}Here he builded a desperate edifice whose foundations were his social talents; and it was with quaint self-abhorrence he often noted how the telling of a smutty jest or the insistence upon a manifestly superfluous glass of wine had purchased from some properly tickled magnate a much desiderated "tip."
10041-8{_}1323{_}And presently these tips misled him.
10041-8{_}1324{_}So the colonel borrowed from "Patricia's account."
10041-8{_}1325{_}And on this occasion he guessed correctly.
10041-8{_}1326{_}And then he stumbled upon such a chance for reinvestment as does not often arrive.
10041-8{_}1327{_}And so he borrowed a trifle more in common justice to Patricia....     VIII   When those then famous warriors, Colonel Gaynor and Captain Green, were obstinately fighting extradition in Quebec; when in Washington the Senate was wording a suitable resolution wherewith to congratulate Cuba upon that island's brand-new independence; and when Messieurs Fitzsimmons and Jeffries were making amicable arrangements in San Francisco to fight for the world's championship:--at this remote time, in Chicago (on the same day, indeed, that in this very city Mr. S.E.
10041-8{_}1328{_}Gross was legally declared the author of a play called Cyrano de Bergerac), the Sons of the Colonial Governors opened their tenth biennial convention.
10041-8{_}1329{_}You may depend upon it that Colonel Rudolph Musgrave represented the Lichfield chapter.
10041-8{_}1330{_}It was two days later the telegram arrived.
10041-8{_}1331{_}It read:    _Agatha very ill come to me roger in perfect health._   PATRICIA.
10041-8{_}1332{_}He noted how with Stapyltonian thrift Patricia telegraphed ten words precisely....  And when he had reached home, late in the evening, the colonel, not having taken his bunch of keys with him, laid down his dress-suit case on the dark porch, and reached out one hand to the door-bell.
10041-8{_}1333{_}He found it muffled with some flimsy, gritty fabric.
10041-8{_}1334{_}He did not ring.
10041-8{_}1335{_}Upon the porch was a rustic bench.
10041-8{_}1336{_}He sat upon it for a quarter of an hour--precisely where he had first talked with Agatha about Patricia's first coming to Lichfield.... Once the door of a house across the street was opened, with a widening gush of amber light wherein he saw three women fitting wraps about them.
10041-8{_}1337{_}One of them was adjusting a lace scarf above her hair.
10041-8{_}1338{_}"No, we're not a _bit_ afraid--Just around the corner, you know--_Such_ a pleasant evening----" Their voices carried far in the still night.
10041-8{_}1339{_}Rudolph Musgrave was not thinking of anything.
10041-8{_}1340{_}Presently he went around through the side entrance, and thus came into the kitchen, where the old mulattress, Virginia, was sitting alone.
10041-8{_}1341{_}The room was very hot....
10041-8{_}1342{_}In Agatha's time supper would have been cooked upon the gas-range in the cellar, he reflected.... Virginia had risen and made as though to take his dress-suit case, her pleasant yellow face as imperturbable as an idol's.
10041-8{_}1343{_}"No--don't bother, Virginia," said Colonel Musgrave.
10041-8{_}1344{_}He met Patricia in the dining-room, on her way to the kitchen.
10041-8{_}1345{_}She had not chosen--as even the most sensible of us will instinctively decline to do--to vex the quiet of a house wherein death was by ringing a bell.
10041-8{_}1346{_}Holding his hand in hers, fondling it as she talked, Patricia told how three nights before Miss Agatha had been "queer, you know," at supper.
10041-8{_}1347{_}Patricia had not liked to leave her, but it was the night of the Woman's Club's second Whist Tournament.
10041-8{_}1348{_}And Virginia had promised to watch Miss Agatha.
10041-8{_}1349{_}And, anyhow, Miss Agatha had gone to bed before Patricia left the house, and _anybody_ would have thought she was going to sleep all night.
10041-8{_}1350{_}And, in fine, Patricia's return at a drizzling half-past eleven had found Miss Agatha sitting in the garden, in her night-dress only, weeping over fancied grievances--and Virginia asleep in the kitchen.
10041-8{_}1351{_}And Agatha had died that afternoon of pneumonia.
10041-8{_}1352{_}Even in the last half-stupor she was asking always when would Rudolph come?
10041-8{_}1353{_}Patricia told him....  Rudolph Musgrave did not say anything.
10041-8{_}1354{_}Without any apparent emotion he put Patricia aside, much as he did the dress-suit case which he had forgotten to lay down until Patricia had ended her recital.
10041-8{_}1355{_}He went upstairs--to the front room, Patricia's bedroom.
10041-8{_}1356{_}Patricia followed him.
10041-8{_}1357{_}Agatha's body lay upon the bed, with a sheet over all.
10041-8{_}1358{_}The undertaker's skill had arranged everything with smug and horrible tranquillity.
10041-8{_}1359{_}Rudolph Musgrave remembered he was forty-six years old; and when in all these years had there been a moment when Agatha--the real Agatha--had not known that what he had done was self-evidently correct, because otherwise Rudolph would not have done it?
10041-8{_}1360{_}"I trust you enjoyed your whist-game, Patricia."
10041-8{_}1361{_}"Well, I couldn't help it.
10041-8{_}1362{_}I'm not running a sanitarium.
10041-8{_}1363{_}I wasn't responsible for her eternal drinking."
10041-8{_}1364{_}The words skipped out of either mouth like gleeful little devils.
10041-8{_}1365{_}Then both were afraid, and both were as icily tranquil as the thing upon the bed.
10041-8{_}1366{_}You could not hear anything except the clock upon the mantel.
10041-8{_}1367{_}Colonel Musgrave went to the mantel, opened the clock, and with an odd deliberation removed the pendulum from its hook.
10041-8{_}1368{_}Followed one metallic gasp, as of indignation, and then silence.
10041-8{_}1369{_}He spoke, still staring at the clock, his back turned to Patricia.
10041-8{_}1370{_}"You must be utterly worn out.
10041-8{_}1371{_}You had better go to bed."
10041-8{_}1372{_}He shifted by the fraction of an inch the old-fashioned "hand-colored" daguerreotype of his father in Confederate uniform.
10041-8{_}1373{_}"Please don't wear that black dress again.
10041-8{_}1374{_}It is no cause for mourning that we are rid of an encumbrance."
10041-8{_}1375{_}Behind him, very far away, it seemed, he heard Patricia wailing, "Olaf----!"
10041-8{_}1376{_}Colonel Musgrave turned without any haste.
10041-8{_}1377{_}"Please go," he said, and appeared to plead with her.
10041-8{_}1378{_}"You must be frightfully tired.
10041-8{_}1379{_}I am sorry that I was not here.
10041-8{_}1380{_}I seem always to evade my responsibilities, somehow--"  Then he began to laugh.
10041-8{_}1381{_}"It _is_ rather amusing, after all.
10041-8{_}1382{_}Agatha was the most noble person I have ever known.
10041-8{_}1383{_}The--this habit of hers to which you have alluded was not a part of her.
10041-8{_}1384{_}And I loved Agatha.
10041-8{_}1385{_}And I suppose loving is not altogether dependent upon logic.
10041-8{_}1386{_}In any event, I loved Agatha.
10041-8{_}1387{_}And when I came back to her I had come home, somehow--wherever she might be at the time.
10041-8{_}1388{_}That has been true, oh, ever since I can remember--"  He touched the dead hand now.
10041-8{_}1389{_}"Please go!"
10041-8{_}1390{_}he said, and he did not look toward Patricia.
10041-8{_}1391{_}"For Agatha loved me better than she did God, you know.
10041-8{_}1392{_}The curse was born in her.
10041-8{_}1393{_}She had to pay for what those dead, soft-handed Musgraves did.
10041-8{_}1394{_}That is why her hands are so cold now.
10041-8{_}1395{_}She had to pay for the privilege of being a Musgrave, you see.
10041-8{_}1396{_}But then we cannot always pick and choose as to what we prefer to be."
10041-8{_}1397{_}"Oh, yes, of course, it is all my fault.
10041-8{_}1398{_}Everything is my fault.
10041-8{_}1399{_}But God knows what would have become of you and your Agatha if it hadn't been for me.
10041-8{_}1400{_}Oh!
10041-8{_}1401{_}oh!"
10041-8{_}1402{_}Patricia wailed.
10041-8{_}1403{_}"I was a child and I hadn't any better sense, and I married you, and you've been living off my money ever since!
10041-8{_}1404{_}There hasn't been a Christmas present or a funeral wreath bought in this house since I came into it I didn't pick out and pay for out of my own pocket.
10041-8{_}1405{_}And all the thanks I get for it is this perpetual fault-finding, and I wish I was dead like this poor saint here.
10041-8{_}1406{_}She spent her life slaving for you.
10041-8{_}1407{_}And what thanks did she get for it?
10041-8{_}1408{_}Oh, you ought to go down on your knees, Rudolph Musgrave--!"
10041-8{_}1409{_}"Please leave," he said.
10041-8{_}1410{_}"I will leave when I feel like it, and not a single minute before, and you might just as well understand as much.
10041-8{_}1411{_}You _have_ been living off my money.
10041-8{_}1412{_}Oh, you needn't go to the trouble of lying.
10041-8{_}1413{_}And she did too.
10041-8{_}1414{_}And she hated me, she always hated me, because I had been fool enough to marry you, and she carried on like a lunatic more than half the time, and I always pretended not to notice it, and this is my reward for trying to behave like a lady."
10041-8{_}1415{_}Patricia tossed her head.
10041-8{_}1416{_}"Yes, and you needn't look at me as if I were some sort of a bug you hadn't ever seen before and didn't approve of, because I've seen you try that high-and-mighty trick too often for it to work with me."
10041-8{_}1417{_}Patricia stood now beneath the Stuart portrait of young Gerald Musgrave.
10041-8{_}1418{_}She had insisted, long ago, that it be hung in her own bedroom--"because it was through that beautiful boy we first got really acquainted, Olaf."
10041-8{_}1419{_}The boy smiles at you from the canvas, smiles ambiguously, as the colonel now noted.
10041-8{_}1420{_}"I think you had better go," said Colonel Musgrave.
10041-8{_}1421{_}"Please go, Patricia, before I murder you."
10041-8{_}1422{_}She saw that he was speaking in perfect earnest.
10041-8{_}1423{_}IX   Rudolph Musgrave sat all night beside the body.
10041-8{_}1424{_}He had declined to speak with innumerable sympathetic cousins--Vartreys and Fentons and Allardyces and Musgraves, to the fifth and sixth remove--who had come from all quarters, with visiting-cards and low-voiced requests to be informed "if there is anything we can possibly do."
10041-8{_}1425{_}Rudolph Musgrave sat all night beside the body.
10041-8{_}1426{_}He had not any strength for anger now, and hardly for grief, Agatha had been his charge; and the fact that he had never plucked up courage to allude to her practises was now an enormity in which he could not quite believe.
10041-8{_}1427{_}His cowardice and its fruitage confronted him, and frightened him into a panic frenzy of remorse.
10041-8{_}1428{_}Agatha had been his charge; and he had entrusted the stewardship to Patricia.
10041-8{_}1429{_}Between them--that Patricia might have her card-game, that he might sit upon a platform for an hour or two with a half-dozen other pompous fools--they had let Agatha die.
10041-8{_}1430{_}There was no mercy in him for Patricia or for himself.
10041-8{_}1431{_}He wished Patricia had been a man.
10041-8{_}1432{_}Had any man --an emperor or a coal-heaver, it would not have mattered--spoken as Patricia had done within the moment, here, within arm's reach of the poor flesh that had been Agatha's, Rudolph Musgrave would have known his duty.
10041-8{_}1433{_}But, according to his code, it was not permitted to be discourteous to a woman....
10041-8{_}1434{_}He caught himself with grotesque meanness wishing that Agatha had been there,--privileged by her sex where he was fettered,--she who was so generous of heart and so fiery of tongue at need; and comprehension that Agatha would never abet or adore him any more smote him anew.
10041-8{_}1435{_}*       *       *       *       *  And chance reserved for him more poignant torture.
10041-8{_}1436{_}Next day, while Rudolph Musgrave was making out the list of honorary pall-bearers, the postman brought a letter which had been forwarded from Chicago.
10041-8{_}1437{_}It was from Agatha, written upon the morning of that day wherein later she had been, as Patricia phrased it, "queer, you know."
10041-8{_}1438{_}He found it wildly droll to puzzle out those "crossed" four sheets of trivialities written in an Italian hand so minute and orderly that the finished page suggested a fly-screen.
10041-8{_}1439{_}He had so often remonstrated with Agatha about her penuriousness as concerned stationery.
10041-8{_}1440{_}"Selina Brice & the Rev'd Henry Anstruther, who now has a church in Seattle, have announced their engagement.
10041-8{_}1441{_}Stanley Haggage has gone to Alabama to marry Leonora Bright, who moved from here a year ago.
10041-8{_}1442{_}They are both as poor as church mice, & I think marriage in such a case an unwise step for anyone.
10041-8{_}1443{_}It brings cares & anxieties enough any way, without starting out with poverty to increase and render deeper every trouble...."  Such was the tenor of Agatha's last letter, of the last self-expression of that effigy upstairs who (you could see) knew everything and was not discontent.
10041-8{_}1444{_}Here the dead spoke, omniscient; and told you that Stanley Haggage had gone to Alabama, and that marriage brought new cares and anxieties.
10041-8{_}1445{_}"I cannot laugh," said Rudolph Musgrave, aloud.
10041-8{_}1446{_}"I know the jest deserves it.
10041-8{_}1447{_}But I cannot laugh, because my upper lip seems to be made of leather and I can't move it.
10041-8{_}1448{_}And, besides, I loved Agatha to a degree which only You and I have ever known of.
10041-8{_}1449{_}She never understood quite how I loved her.
10041-8{_}1450{_}Oh, won't You make her understand just how I loved her?
10041-8{_}1451{_}For Agatha is dead, because You wanted her to be dead, and I have never told her how much I loved her, and now I cannot ever tell her how much I loved her.
10041-8{_}1452{_}Oh, won't You please show me that You have made her understand?
10041-8{_}1453{_}or else have me struck by lightning?
10041-8{_}1454{_}or do _anything_....?"
10041-8{_}1455{_}Nothing was done.
10041-8{_}1456{_}X   And afterward Rudolph Musgrave and his wife met amicably, and without reference to their last talk.
10041-8{_}1457{_}Patricia wore black-and-white for some six months, and Colonel Musgrave accepted the compromise tacitly.
10041-8{_}1458{_}All passed with perfect smoothness between them; and anyone in Lichfield would have told you that the Musgraves were a model couple.
10041-8{_}1459{_}She called him "Rudolph" now.
10041-8{_}1460{_}"Olaf is such a silly-sounding nickname for two old married people, you know," Patricia estimated.
10041-8{_}1461{_}The colonel negligently said that he supposed it did sound odd.
10041-8{_}1462{_}"Only I don't think Clarice Pendomer would care about coming," he resumed,--for the two were discussing an uncompleted list of the people Patricia was to invite to their first house-party.
10041-8{_}1463{_}"And for heaven's sake, why not?
10041-8{_}1464{_}We always have her to everything."
10041-8{_}1465{_}He could not tell her it was because the Charterises were to be among their guests.
10041-8{_}1466{_}So he said: "Oh, well--!"
10041-8{_}1467{_}"Mrs. C.B.
10041-8{_}1468{_}Pendomer, then"--Patricia wrote the name with a flourish.
10041-8{_}1469{_}"Oh, you jay-bird, I'm not jealous.
10041-8{_}1470{_}Everybody knows you never had any more morals than a tom-cat on the back fence.
10041-8{_}1471{_}It's a lucky thing the boy didn't take after you, isn't it?
10041-8{_}1472{_}He doesn't, not a bit.
10041-8{_}1473{_}No, Harry Pendomer is the puniest black-haired little wretch, whereas your other son, sir, resembles his mother and is in consequence a ravishingly beautiful person of superlative charm--"  He was staring at her so oddly that she paused.
10041-8{_}1474{_}So Patricia was familiar with that old scandal which linked his name with Clarice Pendomer's!
10041-8{_}1475{_}He was wondering if Patricia had married him in the belief that she was marrying a man who, appraised by any standards, had acted infamously.
10041-8{_}1476{_}"I was only thinking you had better ask Judge Allardyce, Patricia.
10041-8{_}1477{_}You see, he is absolutely certain not to come--"         *       *       *       *       *  This year the Musgraves had decided not to spend the spring alone together at Matocton, as they had done the four preceding years.
10041-8{_}1478{_}"It looks so silly," as Patricia pointed out.
10041-8{_}1479{_}And, besides, a house-party is the most economical method,--as she also pointed out, being born a Stapylton--of paying off your social obligations, because you can always ask so many people who, you know, have made other plans, and cannot accept.
10041-8{_}1480{_}*       *       *       *       *  "So we will invite Judge Allardyce, of course," said Patricia.
10041-8{_}1481{_}"I had forgotten his court met in June.
10041-8{_}1482{_}Oh, and Peter Blagden too.
10041-8{_}1483{_}It had slipped my mind his uncle was dead...."  "I learned this morning Mrs. Haggage was to lecture in Louisville on the sixteenth.
10041-8{_}1484{_}She was reading up in the Library, you see--"  "Rudolph, you are the lodestar of my existence.
10041-8{_}1485{_}I will ask her to come on the fourteenth and spend a week.
10041-8{_}1486{_}I never could abide the hag, but she has such a--There!
10041-8{_}1487{_}I've made a big blot right in the middle of 'darling,' and spoiled a perfectly good sheet of paper!...
10041-8{_}1488{_}You'd better mail it at once, though, because the evening-paper may have something in it about her lecture."
10041-8{_}1489{_}XI   Rudolph--"  "Why--er--yes, dear?"
10041-8{_}1490{_}This was after supper, and Patricia was playing solitaire.
10041-8{_}1491{_}Her husband was reading the paper.
10041-8{_}1492{_}"Agatha told me all about Virginia, you know--"  Here Colonel Musgrave frowned.
10041-8{_}1493{_}"It is not a pleasant topic."
10041-8{_}1494{_}"You jay-bird, you behave entirely too much as if you were my grandfather.
10041-8{_}1495{_}As I was saying, Agatha told me all about your uncle and Virginia," Patricia hurried on.
10041-8{_}1496{_}"And how she ran away afterwards, and hid in the woods for three days, and came to your father's plantation, and how your father bought her, and how her son was born, and how her son was lynched--"  "Now, really, Patricia!
10041-8{_}1497{_}Surely there are other matters which may be more profitably discussed."
10041-8{_}1498{_}"Of course.
10041-8{_}1499{_}Now, for instance, why is the King of Hearts the only one that hasn't a moustache?"
10041-8{_}1500{_}Patricia peeped to see what cards lay beneath that monarch, and upon reflection moved the King of Spades into the vacant space.
10041-8{_}1501{_}She was a devotee of solitaire and invariably cheated at it.
10041-8{_}1502{_}She went on, absently: "But don't you see?
10041-8{_}1503{_}That colored boy was your own first cousin, and he was killed for doing exactly what his father had done.
10041-8{_}1504{_}Only they sent the father to the Senate and gave him columns of flubdub and laid him out in state when he died--and they poured kerosene upon the son and burned him alive.
10041-8{_}1505{_}And I believe Virginia thinks that wasn't fair."
10041-8{_}1506{_}"What do you mean?"
10041-8{_}1507{_}"I honestly believe Virginia hates the Musgraves.
10041-8{_}1508{_}She is only a negro, of course, but then she was a mother once--Oh, yes!
10041-8{_}1509{_}all I need is a black eight--" Patricia demanded, "Now look at your brother Hector--the awfully dissipated one that died of an overdose of opiates.
10041-8{_}1510{_}When it happened wasn't Virginia taking care of him?"
10041-8{_}1511{_}"Of course.
10041-8{_}1512{_}She is an invaluable nurse."
10041-8{_}1513{_}"And nobody else was here when Agatha went out into the rain.
10041-8{_}1514{_}Now, what if she had just let Agatha go, without trying to stop her?
10041-8{_}1515{_}It would have been perfectly simple.
10041-8{_}1516{_}So is this.
10041-8{_}1517{_}All I have to do is to take them off now."
10041-8{_}1518{_}Colonel Musgrave negligently returned to his perusal of the afternoon paper.
10041-8{_}1519{_}"You are suggesting--if you will overlook my frankness--the most deplorable sort of nonsense, Patricia."
10041-8{_}1520{_}"I know exactly how Balaam felt," she said, irrelevantly, and fell to shuffling the cards.
10041-8{_}1521{_}"You don't, and you won't, understand that Virginia is a human being.
10041-8{_}1522{_}In any event, I wish you would get rid of her."
10041-8{_}1523{_}"I couldn't decently do that," said Rudolph Musgrave, with careful patience.
10041-8{_}1524{_}"Virginia's faithfulness has been proven by too many years of faithful service.
10041-8{_}1525{_}Nothing more strikingly attests the folly of freeing the negro than the unwillingness of the better class of slaves to leave their former owners--"  "Now you are going to quote a paragraph or so from your Gracious Era.
10041-8{_}1526{_}As if I hadn't read everything you ever wrote!
10041-8{_}1527{_}You are a fearful humbug in some ways, Rudolph."
10041-8{_}1528{_}"And you are a red-headed rattlepate, madam.
10041-8{_}1529{_}But seriously, Patricia, you who were reared in the North are strangely unwilling to concede that we of the South are after all best qualified to deal with the Negro Problem.
10041-8{_}1530{_}We know the negro as you cannot ever know him."
10041-8{_}1531{_}"You!
10041-8{_}1532{_}Oh, God ha' mercy on us!"
10041-8{_}1533{_}mocked Patricia.
10041-8{_}1534{_}"There wasn't any Negro Problem hereabouts, you beautiful idiot, so long as there were any negroes.
10041-8{_}1535{_}Why, to-day there is hardly one full-blooded negro in Lichfield.
10041-8{_}1536{_}There are only a thousand or so of mulattoes who share the blood of people like your Uncle Edward.
10041-8{_}1537{_}And for the most part they take after their white kin, unfortunately.
10041-8{_}1538{_}And there you have the Lichfield Negro Problem in a nutshell.
10041-8{_}1539{_}It is a venerable one and fully set forth in the Bible.
10041-8{_}1540{_}You needn't attempt to argue with me, because you are a ninnyhammer, and I am a second Nestor.
10041-8{_}1541{_}The Holy Scriptures are perfectly explicit as to what happens to the heads of the children and their teeth too."
10041-8{_}1542{_}"I wish you wouldn't jest about such matters--"  "Because it isn't lady-like?
10041-8{_}1543{_}But, Rudolph, you know perfectly well that I am not a lady."
10041-8{_}1544{_}"My dear!"
10041-8{_}1545{_}he cried, in horror that was real, "and what on earth have I said even to suggest--"  "Oh, not a syllable; it isn't at all the sort of thing that your sort _says_ ... And I am not your sort.
10041-8{_}1546{_}I don't know that I altogether wish I were.
10041-8{_}1547{_}But _if_ I were, it would certainly make things easier," Patricia added sharply.
10041-8{_}1548{_}"My dear--!"
10041-8{_}1549{_}he again protested.
10041-8{_}1550{_}"Now, candidly, Rudolph"--relinquishing the game, she fell to shuffling the cards--"just count up the number of times this month that my--oh, well!
10041-8{_}1551{_}I really don't know what to call it except my deplorable omission in failing to be born a lady--has seemed to you to yank the very last rag off the gooseberry-bush?"
10041-8{_}1552{_}He scoffed.
10041-8{_}1553{_}"What nonsense!
10041-8{_}1554{_}Although, of course, Patricia--"  She nodded, mischief in her brightly-colored tiny face.
10041-8{_}1555{_}"Yes, that is just your attitude, you beautiful idiot."
10041-8{_}1556{_}"--although, of course--now, quite honestly, Patricia, I have occasionally wished that you would not speak of sacred and--er, physical and sociological matters in exactly the tone in which--well!
10041-8{_}1557{_}in which you sometimes do speak of them.
10041-8{_}1558{_}It may sound old-fashioned, but I have always believed that decency is quite as important in mental affairs as it is in physical ones, and that as a consequence, a gentlewoman should always clothe her thoughts with at least the same care she accords her body.
10041-8{_}1559{_}Oh, don't misunderstand me!
10041-8{_}1560{_}Of course it doesn't do any harm, my dear, between us.
10041-8{_}1561{_}But outside--you see, for people to know that you think about such things must necessarily give them a false opinion of you."
10041-8{_}1562{_}Patricia meditated.
10041-8{_}1563{_}She said, with utter solemnity, "Anathema maranatha!
10041-8{_}1564{_}oh, hell to damn!
10041-8{_}1565{_}may the noses of all respectable people be turned upside down and jackasses dance eternally upon their grandmothers' graves!"
10041-8{_}1566{_}"Patricia--!"
10041-8{_}1567{_}cried a shocked colonel.
10041-8{_}1568{_}"I mean every syllable of it.
10041-8{_}1569{_}No, Rudolph; _I_ can't help it if the vinaigretted beauties of your boyhood were unabridged dictionaries of prudery.
10041-8{_}1570{_}You see, I know almost all the swearwords there are.
10041-8{_}1571{_}And I read the newspapers, and medical books, and even the things that boys chalk up on fences.
10041-8{_}1572{_}In consequence I am not a bit whiteminded, because if you use your mind at all it gets more or less dingy, just like using anything else."
10041-8{_}1573{_}He could not help but laugh, much as he disapproved.
10041-8{_}1574{_}Patricia fluttered and, as a wren might have done, perched presently upon his knee.
10041-8{_}1575{_}"Rudolph, can't you laugh more often, and not devote so much time to tracing out the genealogies of those silly people, and being so tediously beautiful and good?"
10041-8{_}1576{_}she asked, and with a hint of seriousness.
10041-8{_}1577{_}"Rudolph, you don't know how I would adore you if you would rob a church or cut somebody's throat in an alley, and tell me all about it because you knew I wouldn't betray you.
10041-8{_}1578{_}You are so infernally respectable in everything you do!
10041-8{_}1579{_}How did you come to bully me that day at the Library?
10041-8{_}1580{_}It seems almost as if those two were different people... doesn't it, Rudolph?"
10041-8{_}1581{_}"My dear," the colonel said whimsically, "I am afraid we are rather like the shepherdess and the chimney-sweep of the fable I read you very long ago.
10041-8{_}1582{_}We climbed up so far that we could see the stars, once, very long ago, Patricia, and we have come back to live upon the parlor table.
10041-8{_}1583{_}I suppose it happens to all the little china people."
10041-8{_}1584{_}She took his meaning.
10041-8{_}1585{_}Each was aware of an odd sense of intimacy.
10041-8{_}1586{_}"Everything we have to be glad for now, Rudolph, is the rivet in grandfather's neck.
10041-8{_}1587{_}It is rather a fiasco, isn't it?"
10041-8{_}1588{_}"Eh, there are all sorts of rivets, Patricia.
10041-8{_}1589{_}And the thing one cannot do because one is what one is, need not be necessarily a cause for grief."
10041-8{_}1590{_}XII   It was excellent to see Jack Charteris again, as Colonel Musgrave did within a few days of this.
10041-8{_}1591{_}Musgrave was unreasonably fond of the novelist and frankly confessed it would be as preposterous to connect Charteris with any of the accepted standards of morality as it would be to judge an artesian-well from the standpoint of ethics.
10041-8{_}1592{_}Anne was not yet in Lichfield.
10041-8{_}1593{_}She had broken the journey to visit a maternal grand-aunt and some Virginia cousins, in Richmond, Charteris explained, and was to come thence to Matocton.
10041-8{_}1594{_}"And so you have acquired a boy and, by my soul, a very handsome wife, Rudolph?"
10041-8{_}1595{_}"It is sufficiently notorious," said Colonel Musgrave.
10041-8{_}1596{_}"Yes, we are quite absurdly happy."
10041-8{_}1597{_}He laughed and added: "Patricia--but you don't know her droll way of putting things--says that the only rational complaint I can advance against her is her habit of rushing into a hospital every month or so and having a section or two of her person removed by surgeons.
10041-8{_}1598{_}It worries me,--only, of course, it is not the sort of thing you can talk about.
10041-8{_}1599{_}And, as Patricia says, it _is_ an unpleasant thing to realize that your wife is not leaving you through the ordinary channels of death or of type-written decrees of the court, but only in vulgar fractions, as it were--"  "Please don't be quite so brutal, Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1600{_}It is not becoming in a Musgrave of Matocton to speak of women in any tone other than the most honeyed accents of chivalry."
10041-8{_}1601{_}"Oh, I was only quoting Patricia," the colonel largely said, "and--er--Jack," he continued.
10041-8{_}1602{_}"By the way, Jack, Clarice Pendomer will be at Matocton--"  "I rejoice in her good luck," said Charteris, equably.  "
10041-8{_}1603{_}--and--well!
10041-8{_}1604{_}I was wondering--?"
10041-8{_}1605{_}"I can assure you that there will be no--trouble.
10041-8{_}1606{_}That skeleton is safely locked in its closet, and the key to that closet is missing--more thanks to you.
10041-8{_}1607{_}You acted very nobly in the whole affair, Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1608{_}I wish I could do things like that.
10041-8{_}1609{_}As it is, of course, I shall always detest you for having been able to do it."
10041-8{_}1610{_}Charteris said, thereafter: "I shall always envy you, though, Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1611{_}No other man I know has ever attained the good old troubadourish ideal of _domnei_--that love which rather abhors than otherwise the notion of possessing its object.
10041-8{_}1612{_}I still believe it was a distinct relief to a certain military officer, whose name we need not mention, when Anne decided not to marry you."
10041-8{_}1613{_}The colonel grinned, a trifle consciously.
10041-8{_}1614{_}"Well, Anne meant youth, you comprehend, and all the things we then believed in, Jack.
10041-8{_}1615{_}It would have been decidedly difficult to live up to such a contract, and--as it were--to fulfil every one of the implied specifications!"
10041-8{_}1616{_}"And yet"--here Charteris flicked his cigarette--"Anne ruled in the stead of Aline Van Orden.
10041-8{_}1617{_}And Aline, in turn, had followed Clarice Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1618{_}And before the coming of Clarice had Pauline Romeyne, whom time has converted into Polly Ashmeade, reigned in the land--"  "Don't be an ass!"
10041-8{_}1619{_}the colonel pleaded; and then observed, inconsequently: "I can't somehow quite realize Aline is dead.
10041-8{_}1620{_}Lord, Lord, the letters that I wrote to her!
10041-8{_}1621{_}She sent them all back, you know, in genuine romantic fashion, after we had quarreled.
10041-8{_}1622{_}I found those boyish ravings only the other day in my father's desk at Matocton, and skimmed them over.
10041-8{_}1623{_}I shall read them through some day and appropriately meditate over life's mysteries that are too sad for tears."
10041-8{_}1624{_}He meditated now.
10041-8{_}1625{_}"It wouldn't be quite equitable, Jack," the colonel summed it up, "if the Aline I loved--no, I don't mean the real woman, the one you and all the other people knew, the one that married the enterprising brewer and died five years ago--were not waiting for me somewhere.
10041-8{_}1626{_}I can't express just what I mean, but you will understand, I know--?"
10041-8{_}1627{_}"That heaven is necessarily run on a Mohammedan basis?
10041-8{_}1628{_}Why, of course," said Mr. Charteris.
10041-8{_}1629{_}"Heaven, as I apprehend it, is a place where we shall live eternally among those ladies of old years who never condescended actually to inhabit any realm more tangible than that of our boyish fancies.
10041-8{_}1630{_}It is the obvious definition; and I defy you to evolve a more enticing allurement toward becoming a deacon."
10041-8{_}1631{_}"You romancers are privileged to talk nonsense anywhere," the colonel estimated, "and I suppose that in the Lichfield you have made famous, Jack, you have a double right."
10041-8{_}1632{_}"Ah, but I never wrote a line concerning Lichfield.
10041-8{_}1633{_}I only wrote about the Lichfield whose existence you continue to believe in, in spite of the fact that you are actually living in the real Lichfield," Charteris returned.
10041-8{_}1634{_}"The vitality of the legend is wonderful."
10041-8{_}1635{_}He cocked his head to one side--an habitual gesture with Charteris--and the colonel noted, as he had often done before, how extraordinarily reminiscent Jack was of a dried-up, quizzical black parrot.
10041-8{_}1636{_}Said Charteris:  "I love to serve that legend.
10041-8{_}1637{_}I love to prattle of 'ole Marster' and 'ole Miss,' and throw in a sprinkling of 'mockin'-buds' and 'hants' and 'horg-killing time,' and of sweeping animadversions as to all 'free niggers'; and to narrate how 'de quality use ter cum'--you spell it c-u-m because that looks so convincingly like dialect--'ter de gret hous.'
10041-8{_}1638{_}Those are the main ingredients.
10041-8{_}1639{_}And, as for the unavoidable love-interest--" Charteris paused, grinned, and pleasantly resumed: "Why, jes arter dat, suh, a hut Yankee cap'en, whar some uv our folks done shoot in de laig, wuz lef on de road fer daid--a quite notorious custom on the part of all Northern armies--un Young Miss had him fotch up ter de gret hous, un nuss im same's he one uv de fambly, un dem two jes fit un argufy scanlous un never spicion huccum dey's in love wid each othuh till de War's ovuh.
10041-8{_}1640{_}And there you are!
10041-8{_}1641{_}I need not mention that during the tale's progress it is necessary to introduce at least one favorable mention of Lincoln, arrange a duel 'in de low grouns' immediately after day-break, and have the family silver interred in the back garden, because these points will naturally suggest themselves."
10041-8{_}1642{_}"Jack, Jack!"
10041-8{_}1643{_}the colonel cried, "it is an ill bird that fouls its own nest."
10041-8{_}1644{_}"But, believe me, I don't at heart," said Charteris, in a queer earnest voice.
10041-8{_}1645{_}"There is a sardonic imp inside me that makes me jeer at the commoner tricks of the trade--and yet when I am practising that trade, when I am writing of those tender-hearted, brave and gracious men and women, and of those dear old darkies, I very often write with tears in my eyes.
10041-8{_}1646{_}I tell you this with careful airiness because it is true and because it would embarrass me so horribly if you believed it."
10041-8{_}1647{_}Then he was off upon another tack.
10041-8{_}1648{_}"And wherein, pray, have I harmed Lichfield by imagining a dream city situated half way between Atlantis and Avalon and peopled with superhuman persons--and by having called this city Lichfield?
10041-8{_}1649{_}The portrait did not only flatter Lichfield, it flattered human nature.
10041-8{_}1650{_}So, naturally, it pleased everybody.
10041-8{_}1651{_}Yes, that, I take it, is the true secret of romance--to induce the momentary delusion that humanity is a superhuman race, profuse in aspiration, and prodigal in the exercise of glorious virtues and stupendous vices.
10041-8{_}1652{_}As a matter of fact, all human passions are depressingly chicken-hearted, I find.
10041-8{_}1653{_}Were it not for the police court records, I would pessimistically insist that all of us elect to love one person and to hate another with very much the same enthusiasm that we display in expressing a preference for rare roast beef as compared with the outside slice.
10041-8{_}1654{_}Oh, really, Rudolph, you have no notion how salutary it is to the self-esteem of us romanticists to run across, even nowadays, an occasional breach of the peace.
10041-8{_}1655{_}For then sometimes--when the coachman obligingly cuts the butler's throat in the back-alley, say--we actually presume to think for a moment that our profession is almost as honest as that of making counterfeit money...."  The colonel did not interrupt his brief pause of meditation.
10041-8{_}1656{_}Then the novelist said:  "Why, no; if I were ever really to attempt a tale of Lichfield, I would not write a romance but a tragedy.
10041-8{_}1657{_}I think that I would call my tragedy _Futility_, for it would mirror the life of Lichfield with unengaging candor; and, as a consequence, people would complain that my tragedy lacked sustained interest, and that its participants were inconsistent; that it had no ordered plot, no startling incidents, no high endeavors, and no especial aim; and that it was equally deficient in all time-hallowed provocatives of either laughter or tears.
10041-8{_}1658{_}For very few people would understand that a life such as this, when rightly viewed, is the most pathetic tragedy conceivable."
10041-8{_}1659{_}"Oh, come, now, Jack!
10041-8{_}1660{_}come, recollect that your reasoning powers are almost as worthy of employment as your rhetorical abilities!
10041-8{_}1661{_}We are not quite so bad as that, you know.
10041-8{_}1662{_}We may be a little behind the times in Lichfield; we certainly let well enough alone, and we take things pretty much as they come; but we meddle with nobody, and, after all, we don't do any especial harm."
10041-8{_}1663{_}"We don't do anything whatever in especial, Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1664{_}That would be precisely the theme of my story of the real Lichfield if I were ever bold enough to write it.
10041-8{_}1665{_}There seems to be a sort of blight upon Lichfield.
10041-8{_}1666{_}Oh, yes!
10041-8{_}1667{_}it would be unfair, perhaps, to contrast it with the bigger Southern cities, like Richmond and Atlanta and New Orleans; but even the inhabitants of smaller Southern towns are beginning to buy excursion tickets, and thereby ascertain that the twentieth century has really begun.
10041-8{_}1668{_}Yes, it is only in Lichfield I can detect the raw stuff of a genuine tragedy; for, depend upon it, Rudolph, the most pathetic tragedy in life is to get nothing in particular out of it."
10041-8{_}1669{_}"But, for my part, I don't see what you are driving at," the colonel stoutly said.
10041-8{_}1670{_}And Charteris only laughed.
10041-8{_}1671{_}"And I hardly expected you to do so, Rudolph--or not yet, at least."
10041-8{_}1672{_}PART FIVE - SOUVENIR    "I am contented by remembrances--   Dreams of dead passions, wraiths of vanished times,   Fragments of vows, and by-ends of old rhymes--   Flotsam and jetsam tumbling in the seas   Whereon, long since, put forth our argosies   Which, bent on traffic in the Isles of Love,   Lie foundered somewhere in some firth thereof,   Encradled by eternal silences."
10041-8{_}1673{_}"Thus, having come to naked bankruptcy,   Let us part friends, as thrifty tradesmen do   When common ventures fail, for it may be   These battered oaths and rhymes may yet ring true   To some fair woman's hearing, so that she   Will listen and think of love, and I of you."
10041-8{_}1674{_}F. Ashcroft Wheeler.
10041-8{_}1675{_}_Revisions_.
10041-8{_}1676{_}I   When the _Reliance_, the _Constitution_ and the _Columbia_ were holding trial races off Newport to decide which one of these yachts should defend the _America's_ cup; when the tone of the Japanese press as to Russia's actions in Manchuria was beginning to grow ominous; when the Jews of America were drafting a petition to the Czar; and when it was rumored that the health of Pope Leo XIII was commencing to fail:--at this remote time, the Musgraves gave their first house-party.
10041-8{_}1677{_}And at this period Colonel Musgrave noted and admired the apparent unconcern with which John Charteris and Clarice Pendomer encountered at Matocton.
10041-8{_}1678{_}And at this period Colonel Musgrave noted with approval the intimacy which was, obviously, flourishing between the little novelist and Patricia.
10041-8{_}1679{_}Also Colonel Musgrave had presently good reason to lament a contretemps, over which he was sulking when Mrs. Pendomer rustled to her seat at the breakfast-table, with a shortness of breath that was partly due to the stairs, and in part attributable to her youthful dress, which fitted a trifle too perfectly.
10041-8{_}1680{_}"Waffles?"
10041-8{_}1681{_}said Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1682{_}"At my age and weight the first is an experiment and the fifth an amiable indiscretion of which I am invariably guilty.
10041-8{_}1683{_}Sugar, please."
10041-8{_}1684{_}She yawned, and reached a generously-proportioned arm toward the sugar-bowl.
10041-8{_}1685{_}"Yes, that will do, Pilkins."
10041-8{_}1686{_}Colonel Musgrave--since the remainder of his house-party had already breakfasted--raised his fine eyes toward the chandelier, and sighed, as Pilkins demurely closed the dining-room door.
10041-8{_}1687{_}Leander Pilkins--butler for a long while now to the Musgraves of Matocton--would here, if space permitted, be the subject of an encomium.
10041-8{_}1688{_}Leander Pilkins was in Lichfield considered to be, upon the whole, the handsomest man whom Lichfield had produced; for this quadroon's skin was like old ivory, and his profile would have done credit to an emperor.
10041-8{_}1689{_}His terrapin is still spoken of in Lichfield as people in less favored localities speak of the Golden Age, and his mayonnaise (boasts Lichfield) would have compelled an Olympian to plead for a second helping.
10041-8{_}1690{_}For the rest, his deportment in all functions of butlership is best described as super-Chesterfieldian; and, indeed, he was generally known to be a byblow of Captain Beverley Musgrave's, who in his day was Lichfield's arbiter as touched the social graces.
10041-8{_}1691{_}And so, no more of Pilkins.
10041-8{_}1692{_}Mrs. Pendomer partook of chops.
10041-8{_}1693{_}"Is this remorse," she queried, "or a convivially induced requirement for bromides?

10041-8{_}1695{_}"It is neither," said Colonel Musgrave, and almost snappishly.
10041-8{_}1696{_}Followed an interval of silence.

10041-8{_}1714{_}said he.

10041-8{_}1787{_}A woman never realizes that of her lover, somehow.

10041-8{_}1831{_}II   Thus it shortly came about that Mrs. Pendomer mounted, in meditative mood, to Mrs. Musgrave's rooms; and that Mrs. Pendomer, recovering her breath, entered, without knocking, into a gloom where cologne and menthol and the odor of warm rubber contended for mastery.
10041-8{_}1832{_}For Patricia had decided that she was very ill indeed, and was sobbing softly in bed.
10041-8{_}1833{_}Very calmly, Mrs. Pendomer opened a window, letting in a flood of fresh air and sunshine; very calmly, she drew a chair--a substantial arm-chair--to the bedside, and, very calmly, she began:  "My dear, Rudolph has told me of this ridiculous affair, and--oh, you equally ridiculous girl!"
10041-8{_}1834{_}She removed, with deft fingers, a damp and clinging bandage from about Patricia's head, and patted the back of Patricia's hand, placidly.
10041-8{_}1835{_}Patricia was by this time sitting erect in bed, and her coppery hair was thick about her face, which was colorless; and, altogether, she was very rigid and very indignant and very pretty, and very, very young.
10041-8{_}1836{_}"How dare he tell you--or anybody else!"
10041-8{_}1837{_}she cried.

10041-8{_}1841{_}Mrs. Pendomer hesitated for a moment, and then plunged into the heart of things.
10041-8{_}1842{_}"You are a woman, dear," she said, gently, "though heaven knows it must have been only yesterday you were playing about the nursery--and one of the facts we women must face, eventually, is that man is a polygamous animal.
10041-8{_}1843{_}It is unfortunate, perhaps, but it is true.
10041-8{_}1844{_}Civilization may veneer the fact, but nothing will ever override it, not even in these new horseless carriages.
10041-8{_}1845{_}A man may give his wife the best that is in him--his love, his trust, his life's work--but it is only the best there is left.
10041-8{_}1846{_}We give our hearts; men dole out theirs, as people feed bread to birds, with a crumb for everyone.
10041-8{_}1847{_}His wife has the remnant.
10041-8{_}1848{_}And the best we women can do is to remember we are credibly informed that half a loaf is preferable to no bread at all."
10041-8{_}1849{_}Her face sobered, and she added, pensively: "We might contrive a better universe, we sister women, but this is not permitted us.
10041-8{_}1850{_}So we must take it as it is."
10041-8{_}1851{_}Patricia stirred, as talking died away.
10041-8{_}1852{_}"I don't believe it," said she; and she added, with emphasis: "And, anyhow, I hate that nasty trollop!"
10041-8{_}1853{_}"Ah, but you do believe it."

10041-8{_}1857{_}Patricia frowned, petulantly, and then burst into choking sobs.

10041-8{_}1862{_}Oh, that--that hell-cat!
10041-8{_}1863{_}She's had everything!"
10041-8{_}1864{_}There was an interval, during which Mrs. Pendomer smiled crookedly, and Patricia continued to sob, although at lengthening intervals.

10041-8{_}1869{_}And, considering silence as equivalent to acquiescence, she drew out a letter at hazard, and read aloud:  "'Just a line, woman of all the world, to tell you ... but what have I to tell you, after all?
10041-8{_}1870{_}Only the old, old message, so often told that it seems scarcely worth while to bother the postman about it.
10041-8{_}1871{_}Just three words that innumerable dead lips have whispered, while life was yet good and old people were unreasonable and skies were blue--three words that our unborn children's children will whisper to one another when we too have gone to help the grasses in their growing or to nourish the victorious, swaying hosts of some field of daffodils.
10041-8{_}1872{_}Just three words--that is my message to you, my lady.... Ah, it is weary waiting for a sight of your dear face through these long days that are so much alike and all so empty and colorless!

10041-8{_}1874{_}I want you so, O dearest girl in all the world!

10041-8{_}1877{_}It isn't Colonel Musgrave's voice--quite--is it?"

10041-8{_}1880{_}she cried indignantly; "he loved her!

10041-8{_}1882{_}"Why, yes."

10041-8{_}1884{_}"My dear, it was years ago.
10041-8{_}1885{_}I think time has by this wreaked a vengeance far more bitter than you could ever plan on the woman who, after all, never thought to wrong you.
10041-8{_}1886{_}For the bitterest of all bitter things to a woman--to some women, at least--is to grow old."

10041-8{_}1888{_}"Ah, who will write the tragedy of us women who were 'famous Southern beauties' once?
10041-8{_}1889{_}We were queens of men while our youth lasted, and diarists still prattle charmingly concerning us.
10041-8{_}1890{_}But nothing was expected of us save to be beautiful and to condescend to be made much of, and that is our tragedy.
10041-8{_}1891{_}For very few things, my dear, are more pitiable than the middle-age of the pitiful butterfly woman, whose mind cannot--cannot, because of its very nature--reach to anything higher!
10041-8{_}1892{_}Middle-age strips her of everything--the admiration, the flattery, the shallow merriment--all the little things that her little mind longs for--and other women take her place, in spite of her futile, pitiful efforts to remain young.
10041-8{_}1893{_}And the world goes on as before, and there is a whispering in the moonlit garden, and young people steal off for wholly superfluous glasses of water, and the men give her duty dances, and she is old--ah, so old!--under the rouge and inane smiles and dainty fripperies that caricature her lost youth!
10041-8{_}1894{_}No, my dear, you needn't envy this woman!

10041-8{_}1898{_}"Well," Mrs. Pendomer conceded, drily, "she doesn't get it.
10041-8{_}1899{_}Probably, because she always grows fat, from sheer lack of will-power to resist sloth and gluttony--the only agreeable vices left her; and by no stretch of the imagination can a fat woman be converted into either a pleasing or heroic figure."
10041-8{_}1900{_}Mrs. Pendomer paused for a breathing-space, and smiled, though not very pleasantly.
10041-8{_}1901{_}"It is, doubtless," said she, "a sight for gods--and quite certainly for men--to laugh at, this silly woman striving to regain a vanished frugality of waist.
10041-8{_}1902{_}Yes, I suppose it is amusing--but it is also pitiful.
10041-8{_}1903{_}And it is more pitiful still if she has ever loved a man in the unreasoning way these shallow women sometimes do.
10041-8{_}1904{_}Men age so slowly; the men a girl first knows are young long after she has reached middle-age--yes, they go on dancing cotillions and talking nonsense in the garden, long after she has taken to common-sense shoes.
10041-8{_}1905{_}And the man is still young--and he cares for some other woman, who is young and has all that she has lost--and it seems so unfair!"
10041-8{_}1906{_}said Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1907{_}Patricia regarded her for a moment.
10041-8{_}1908{_}The purple eyes were alert, their glance was hard.
10041-8{_}1909{_}"You seem to know all about this woman," Patricia began, in a level voice.
10041-8{_}1910{_}"I have heard, of course, what everyone in Lichfield whispers about you and Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1911{_}I have even teased Rudolph about it, but until to-day I had believed it was a lie."
10041-8{_}1912{_}"It is often a mistake to indulge in uncommon opinions," said Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1913{_}"You get more fun and interest out of it, I don't deny, but the bill, my dear, is unconscionable."
10041-8{_}1914{_}"So!
10041-8{_}1915{_}you confess it!"
10041-8{_}1916{_}"My dear, and who am I to stand aside like a coward and see you make a mountain of this boy-and-girl affair--an affair which Rudolph and I had practically forgotten--oh, years ago!--until to-day?
10041-8{_}1917{_}Why--why, you _can't_ be jealous of me!"
10041-8{_}1918{_}Mrs. Pendomer concluded, half-mockingly.
10041-8{_}1919{_}Patricia regarded her with deliberation.
10041-8{_}1920{_}In the windy sunlight, Mrs. Pendomer was a well-preserved woman, but, unmistakably, preserved; moreover, there was a great deal of her, and her nose was in need of a judicious application of powder, of which there was a superfluity behind her ears.
10041-8{_}1921{_}Was this the siren Patricia had dreaded?
10041-8{_}1922{_}Patricia clearly perceived that, whatever had been her husband's relations with this woman, he had been manifestly entrapped into the imbroglio--a victim to Mrs. Pendomer's inordinate love of attention, which was, indeed, tolerably notorious; and Patricia's anger against Rudolph Musgrave gave way to a rather contemptuous pity and a half-maternal remorse for not having taken better care of him.
10041-8{_}1923{_}"No," answered Mrs. Pendomer, to her unspoken thought; "no woman could be seriously jealous of me.
10041-8{_}1924{_}Yes, I dare say, I am _passée_ and vain and frivolous and--harmless.
10041-8{_}1925{_}But," she added, meditatively, "you hate me, just the same."
10041-8{_}1926{_}"My dear Mrs. Pendomer----" Patricia began, with cool courtesy; then hesitated.
10041-8{_}1927{_}"Yes," she conceded; "I dare say, it is unreasonable--but I do hate you like the very old Nick."
10041-8{_}1928{_}"Why, then," spoke Mrs. Pendomer, with cheerfulness, "everything is as it should be."
10041-8{_}1929{_}She rose and smiled.
10041-8{_}1930{_}"I am sorry to say I must be leaving Matocton to-day; the Ullwethers are very pressing, and I really don't know how to get out of paying them a visit----"  "So sorry to lose you," cooed Patricia; "but, of course, you know best.
10041-8{_}1931{_}I believe some very good people are visiting the Ullwethers nowadays?"
10041-8{_}1932{_}She extended the letters, blandly.
10041-8{_}1933{_}"May I restore your property?"
10041-8{_}1934{_}she queried, with utmost gentleness.
10041-8{_}1935{_}"Thanks!"
10041-8{_}1936{_}Clarice Pendomer took them, and kissed her hostess, not without tenderness, on the brow.
10041-8{_}1937{_}"My dear, be kind to Rudolph.
10041-8{_}1938{_}He--he is rather an attractive man, you know,--and other women are kind to him.
10041-8{_}1939{_}We of Lichfield have always said that he and Jack Charteris were the most dangerous men that even Lichfield has ever produced----"  "Why, do people really find Mr. Charteris particularly attractive?"
10041-8{_}1940{_}Patricia demanded, so quickly and so innocently that Mrs. Pendomer could not deny herself the glance of a charlatan who applauds his fellow's legerdemain.
10041-8{_}1941{_}And Patricia colored.
10041-8{_}1942{_}"Oh, well--!
10041-8{_}1943{_}You know how Lichfield gossips," said Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1944{_}III   Colonel Musgrave had smoked a preposterous number of unsatisfying cigarettes on the big front porch of Matocton whilst Mrs. Pendomer was absent on her mission; and on her return, flushed and triumphant, he rose in eloquent silence.
10041-8{_}1945{_}"I've done it, Rudolph," said Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1946{_}"Done what?"
10041-8{_}1947{_}he queried, blankly.
10041-8{_}1948{_}"Restored what my incomprehensible lawyers call the _status quo_; achieved peace with honor; carried off the spoils of war; and--in short--arranged everything," answered Mrs. Pendomer, and sank into a rustic chair, which creaked admonishingly.
10041-8{_}1949{_}"And all," she added, bringing a fan into play, "without a single falsehood.
10041-8{_}1950{_}_I_ am not to blame if Patricia has jumped at the conclusion that these letters were written to me."
10041-8{_}1951{_}"My word!"
10041-8{_}1952{_}said Rudolph Musgrave, "your methods of restoring domestic peace to a distracted household are, to say the least, original!"
10041-8{_}1953{_}He seated himself, and lighted another cigarette.
10041-8{_}1954{_}"Oh, well, Patricia is not deaf, you know, and she has lived in Lichfield quite a while."
10041-8{_}1955{_}Mrs. Pendomer said abruptly, "I have half a mind to tell you some of the things I know about Aline Van Orden."
10041-8{_}1956{_}"Please don't," said Colonel Musgrave, "for I would inevitably beard you on my own porch and smite you to the door-mat.
10041-8{_}1957{_}And I am hardly young enough for such adventures."
10041-8{_}1958{_}"And poor Aline is dead!
10041-8{_}1959{_}And the rest of us are middle-aged now, Rudolph, and we go in to dinner with the veterans who call us 'Madam,' and we are prominent in charitable enterprises....
10041-8{_}1960{_}But there was a time when we were not exactly hideous in appearance, and men did many mad things for our sakes, and we never lose the memory of that time.
10041-8{_}1961{_}Pleasant memories are among the many privileges of women.
10041-8{_}1962{_}Yes," added Mrs. Pendomer, meditatively, "we derive much the same pleasure from them a cripple does from rearranging the athletic medals he once won, or a starving man from thinking of the many excellent dinners he has eaten; but we can't and we wouldn't part with them, nevertheless."
10041-8{_}1963{_}Rudolph Musgrave, however, had not honored her with much attention, and was puzzling over the more or less incomprehensible situation; and, perceiving this, she ran on, after a little:  "Oh, it worked--it worked beautifully!
10041-8{_}1964{_}You see, she would always have been very jealous of that other woman; but with me it is different.
10041-8{_}1965{_}She has always known that scandalous story about you and me.
10041-8{_}1966{_}And she has always known me as I am--a frivolous and--say, corpulent, for it is a more dignified word--and generally unattractive chaperon; and she can't think of me as ever having been anything else.
10041-8{_}1967{_}Young people never really believe in their elders' youth, Rudolph; at heart, they think we came into the world with crow's-feet and pepper-and-salt hair, all complete.
10041-8{_}1968{_}So, she is only sorry for you now--rather as a mother would be for a naughty child; as for me, she isn't jealous--but," sighed Mrs. Pendomer, "she isn't over-fond of me."
10041-8{_}1969{_}Colonel Musgrave rose to his feet.
10041-8{_}1970{_}"It isn't fair," said he; "the letters were distinctly compromising.
10041-8{_}1971{_}It isn't fair you should shoulder the blame for a woman who was nothing to you.
10041-8{_}1972{_}It isn't fair you should be placed in such a false position."
10041-8{_}1973{_}"What matter?"
10041-8{_}1974{_}pleaded Mrs. Pendomer.
10041-8{_}1975{_}"The letters are mine to burn, if I choose.
10041-8{_}1976{_}I have read one of them, by the way, and it is almost word for word a letter you wrote me a good twenty years ago.
10041-8{_}1977{_}And you re-hashed it for Patricia's benefit too, it seems!
10041-8{_}1978{_}You ought to get a mimeograph.
10041-8{_}1979{_}Oh, very well!
10041-8{_}1980{_}It doesn't matter now, for Patricia will say nothing--or not at least to you," she added.
10041-8{_}1981{_}"Still----" he began.
10041-8{_}1982{_}"Ah, Rudolph, if I want to do a foolish thing, why won't you let me?
10041-8{_}1983{_}What else is a woman for?
10041-8{_}1984{_}They are always doing foolish things.
10041-8{_}1985{_}I have known a woman to throw a man over, because she had seen him without a collar; and I have known another actually to marry a man, because she happened to be in love with him.
10041-8{_}1986{_}I have known a woman to go on wearing pink organdie after she has passed forty, and I have known a woman to go on caring for a man who, she knew, wasn't worth caring for, long after he had forgotten.
10041-8{_}1987{_}We are not brave and sensible, like you men.
10041-8{_}1988{_}So why not let me be foolish, if I want to be?"
10041-8{_}1989{_}"If," said Colonel Musgrave in some perplexity, "I understand one word of this farrago, I will be--qualified in various ways."
10041-8{_}1990{_}"But you don't have to understand," she pleaded.
10041-8{_}1991{_}"You mean--?"
10041-8{_}1992{_}he asked.
10041-8{_}1993{_}"I mean that I was always fond of Aline, anyhow."
10041-8{_}1994{_}"Nonsense!"
10041-8{_}1995{_}And he was conscious, with vexation, that he had undeniably flushed.
10041-8{_}1996{_}"I mean, then, I am a woman, and _I_ understand.
10041-8{_}1997{_}Everything is as near what it should be as is possible while Patricia is seeing so much of--we will call it the artistic temperament."
10041-8{_}1998{_}Mrs. Pendomer shrugged.
10041-8{_}1999{_}"But if I went on in that line you would believe I was jealous.
10041-8{_}2000{_}And heaven knows I am not the least bit so--with the unavoidable qualification that, being a woman, I can't help rising superior to common-sense."
10041-8{_}2001{_}He said, "You mean Jack Charteris--?
10041-8{_}2002{_}But what on earth has he to do with these letters?"
10041-8{_}2003{_}"I don't mean any proper names at all.
10041-8{_}2004{_}I simply mean you are not to undo my work.
10041-8{_}2005{_}It would only signify trouble and dissatisfaction and giving up all this"--she waved her hand lightly toward the lawns of Matocton,--"and it would mean our giving you up, for, you know, you haven't any money of your own, Rudolph.
10041-8{_}2006{_}Ah, Rudolph, we can't give you up!
10041-8{_}2007{_}We need you to lead our Lichfield germans, and to tell us naughty little stories, and keep us amused.
10041-8{_}2008{_}So _please_ be sensible, Rudolph."
10041-8{_}2009{_}"Permit me to point out I firmly believe that silence is the perfectest herald of joy," observed Colonel Musgrave.
10041-8{_}2010{_}"Only I do _not_ understand why you should have dragged John Charteris's name into this ludicrous affair----"  "You really do not understand----?"
10041-8{_}2011{_}But Colonel Musgrave's handsome face declared very plainly that he did not.
10041-8{_}2012{_}"Well," Mrs. Pendomer reflected, "I dare say it is best, upon the whole, you shouldn't.
10041-8{_}2013{_}And now you must excuse me, for I am leaving for the Ullwethers' to-day, and I shan't ever be invited to Matocton again, and I must tell my maid to pack up.
10041-8{_}2014{_}She is a little fool and it will break her heart to be leaving Pilkins.
10041-8{_}2015{_}All human beings are tediously alike.
10041-8{_}2016{_}But, allowing ample time for her to dispose of my best lingerie and of her unavoidable lamentations, I ought to make the six-forty-five.
10041-8{_}2017{_}I have noticed that one usually does--somehow," said Mrs. Pendomer, and seemed to smack of allegories.
10041-8{_}2018{_}And yet it may have been because she knew--as who knew better?--something of that mischief's nature which was now afoot.
10041-8{_}2019{_}IV   The colonel burned the malefic letters that afternoon.
10041-8{_}2020{_}Indeed, the episode set him to ransacking the desk in which Patricia had found them--a desk which, as you have heard, was heaped with the miscellaneous correspondence of the colonel's father dating back a half-century and more.
10041-8{_}2021{_}Much curious matter the colonel discovered there, for "Wild Will" Musgrave's had been a full-blooded career.
10041-8{_}2022{_}And over one packet of letters, in particular, the colonel sat for a long while with an unwontedly troubled face.
10041-8{_}2023{_}PART SIX - BYWAYS    "Cry _Kismet!_ and take heart.
10041-8{_}2024{_}Eros is gone,   Nor may we follow to that loftier air   Olympians breathe.
10041-8{_}2025{_}Take heart, and enter where   A lighter Love, vine-crowned, laughs i' the sun,   Oblivious of tangled webs ill-spun   By ancient wearied weavers, for it may be   His guidance leads to lovers of such as we   And hearts so credulous as to be won.
10041-8{_}2026{_}"Cry _Kismet!_ Put away vain memories   Of all old sorrows and of all old joys,   And learn that life is never quite amiss   So long as unreflective girls and boys   Remember that young lips were meant to kiss,   And hold that laughter is a seemly noise."
10041-8{_}2027{_}PAUL VANDERHOFFEN.
10041-8{_}2028{_}_Egeria Answers._     I   Patricia sat in the great maple-grove that stands behind Matocton, and pondered over a note from her husband, who was in Lichfield superintending the appearance of the July number of the _Lichfield Historical Association's Quarterly Magazine_.
10041-8{_}2029{_}Mr. Charteris lay at her feet, glancing rapidly over a lengthy letter, which was from his wife, in Richmond.
10041-8{_}2030{_}The morning mail was just in, and Patricia had despatched Charteris for her letters, on the plea that the woods were too beautiful to leave, and that Matocton, in the unsettled state which marks the end of the week in a house-party, was intolerable.
10041-8{_}2031{_}She, undoubtedly, was partial to the grove, having spent the last ten mornings there.
10041-8{_}2032{_}Mr. Charteris had overrated her modest literary abilities so far as to ask her advice in certain details of his new book, which was to appear in the autumn, and they had found a vernal solitude, besides being extremely picturesque, to be conducive to the forming of really matured opinions.
10041-8{_}2033{_}Moreover, she was assured that none of the members of the house-party would misunderstand her motives; people were so much less censorious in the country; there was something in the pastoral purity of Nature, seen face to face, which brought out one's noblest instincts, and put an end to all horrid gossip and scandal-mongering.
10041-8{_}2034{_}Didn't Mrs. Barry-Smith think so?
10041-8{_}2035{_}And what was her real opinion of that rumor about the Hardresses, and was the woman as bad as people said she was?
10041-8{_}2036{_}Thus had Patricia spoken in the privacy of her chamber, at that hour when ladies do up their hair for the night, and discourse of mysteries.
10041-8{_}2037{_}It is at this time they are said to babble out their hearts to one another; and so, beyond doubt, this must have been the real state of the case.
10041-8{_}2038{_}As Patricia admitted, she had given up bridge and taken to literature only during the past year.
10041-8{_}2039{_}She might more honestly have said within the last two weeks.
10041-8{_}2040{_}In any event, she now conversed of authors with a fitful persistence like that of an ill-regulated machine.
10041-8{_}2041{_}Her comments were delightfully frank and original, as she had an unusually good memory.
10041-8{_}2042{_}Of two books she was apt to prefer the one with the wider margin, and she was becoming sufficiently familiar with a number of poets to quote them inaccurately.
10041-8{_}2043{_}We have all seen John Charteris's portraits, and most of us have read his books--or at least, the volume entitled _In Old Lichfield_, which caused the _Lichfield Courier-Herald_ to apostrophize its author as a "Child of Genius!
10041-8{_}2044{_}whose ardent soul has sounded the mysteries of life, whose inner vision sweeps over ever widening fields of thought, and whose chiseled phrases continue patriotically to perpetuate the beauty of Lichfield's past."
10041-8{_}2045{_}But for present purposes it is sufficient to say that this jewelsmith of words was slight and dark and hook-nosed, and that his hair was thin, and that he was not ill-favored.
10041-8{_}2046{_}It may be of interest to his admirers--a growing cult--to add that his reason for wearing a mustache in a period of clean-shaven faces was that, without it, his mouth was not pleasant to look upon.
10041-8{_}2047{_}"Heigho!"
10041-8{_}2048{_}Patricia said, at length, with a little laugh; "it is very strange that both of our encumbrances should arrive on the same day!"
10041-8{_}2049{_}"It is unfortunate," Mr. Charteris admitted, lazily; "but the blessed state of matrimony is liable to these mishaps.
10041-8{_}2050{_}Let us be thankful that my wife's whim to visit her aunt has given us, at least, two perfect, golden weeks.
10041-8{_}2051{_}Husbands are like bad pennies; and wives resemble the cat whose adventures have been commemorated by one of our really popular poets.
10041-8{_}2052{_}They always come back."
10041-8{_}2053{_}Patricia communed with herself, and to Charteris seemed, as she sat in the chequered sunlight, far more desirable than a married woman has any right to be.
10041-8{_}2054{_}"I wish--" she began, slowly.
10041-8{_}2055{_}"Oh, but, you know, it was positively criminal negligence not to have included a dozen fairies among my sponsors."
10041-8{_}2056{_}"I too have desiderated this sensible precaution," said Charteris, and laughed his utter comprehension.
10041-8{_}2057{_}"But, after all," he said, and snapped his fingers gaily, "we still have twenty-four hours, Patricia!
10041-8{_}2058{_}Let us forget the crudities of life, and say foolish things to each other.
10041-8{_}2059{_}For I am pastorally inclined this morning, Patricia; I wish to lie at your feet and pipe amorous ditties upon an oaten reed.
10041-8{_}2060{_}Have you such an article about you, Patricia?"
10041-8{_}2061{_}He drew a key-ring from his pocket, and pondered over it.
10041-8{_}2062{_}"Or would you prefer that I whistle into the opening of this door-key, to the effect that we must gather our rose-buds while we may, for Time is still a-flying, fa-la, and that a drear old age, not to mention our spouses, will soon descend upon us, fa-la-di-leero?
10041-8{_}2063{_}A door-key is not Arcadian, Patricia, but it makes a very creditable noise."
10041-8{_}2064{_}"Don't be foolish, _mon ami_!"
10041-8{_}2065{_}she protested, with an indulgent smile.
10041-8{_}2066{_}"I am unhappy."
10041-8{_}2067{_}"Unhappy that I have chanced to fall in love with you, Patricia?
10041-8{_}2068{_}It is an accident which might befall any really intelligent person."
10041-8{_}2069{_}She shrugged her shoulders, ruefully.
10041-8{_}2070{_}"I have done wrong to let you talk to me as you have done of late.
10041-8{_}2071{_}I--oh, Jack, I am afraid!"
10041-8{_}2072{_}Mr. Charteris meditated.
10041-8{_}2073{_}Somewhere in a neighboring thicket a bird trilled out his song--a contented, half-hushed song that called his mate to witness how infinitely blest above all other birds was he.
10041-8{_}2074{_}Mr. Charteris heard him to the end, and languidly made as to applaud; then Mr. Charteris raised his eyebrows.
10041-8{_}2075{_}"Of your husband, Patricia?"
10041-8{_}2076{_}he queried.
10041-8{_}2077{_}"I--Rudolph doesn't bother about me nowadays sufficiently to--notice anything."
10041-8{_}2078{_}Mr. Charteris smiled.
10041-8{_}2079{_}"Of my wife, Patricia?"
10041-8{_}2080{_}"Good gracious, no!
10041-8{_}2081{_}I have not the least doubt you will explain matters satisfactorily to your wife, for I have always heard that practise makes perfect."
10041-8{_}2082{_}Mr. Charteris laughed--a low and very musical laugh.
10041-8{_}2083{_}"Of me, then, Patricia?"
10041-8{_}2084{_}"I--I think it is rather of myself I am afraid.
10041-8{_}2085{_}Oh, I hate you when you smile like that!
10041-8{_}2086{_}You have evil eyes, Jack!
10041-8{_}2087{_}Stop it!
10041-8{_}2088{_}Quit hounding me with your illicit fascinations."
10041-8{_}2089{_}The hand she had raised in threatening fashion fell back into her lap, and she shrugged her shoulders once more.
10041-8{_}2090{_}"My nerves are somewhat upset by the approaching prospect of connubial felicity, I suppose.
10041-8{_}2091{_}Really, though, _mon ami_, your conceit is appalling."
10041-8{_}2092{_}Charteris gave vent to a chuckle, and raised the door-key to his lips.
10041-8{_}2093{_}"When you are quite through your histrionic efforts," he suggested, apologetically, "I will proceed with my amorous pipings.
10041-8{_}2094{_}Really, Patricia, one might fancy you the heroine of a society drama, working up the sympathies of the audience before taking to evil ways.
10041-8{_}2095{_}Surely, you are not about to leave your dear, good, patient husband, Patricia?
10041-8{_}2096{_}Heroines only do that on dark and stormy nights, and in an opera toilette; wearing her best gown seems always to affect a heroine in that way."
10041-8{_}2097{_}Mr. Charteris, at this point, dropped the key-ring, and drew nearer to her; his voice sank to a pleading cadence.
10041-8{_}2098{_}"We are in Arcadia, Patricia; virtue and vice are contraband in this charming country, and must be left at the frontier.
10041-8{_}2099{_}Let us be adorably foolish and happy, my lady, and forget for a little the evil days that approach.
10041-8{_}2100{_}Can you not fancy this to be Arcadia, Patricia?--it requires the merest trifle of imagination.
10041-8{_}2101{_}Listen very carefully, and you will hear the hoofs of fauns rustling among the fallen leaves; they are watching us, Patricia, from behind every tree-bole.
10041-8{_}2102{_}They think you a dryad--the queen of all the dryads, with the most glorious eyes and hair and the most tempting lips in all the forest.
10041-8{_}2103{_}After a little, shaggy, big-thewed ventripotent Pan will grow jealous, and ravish you away from me, as he stole Syrinx from her lover.
10041-8{_}2104{_}You are very beautiful, Patricia; you are quite incredibly beautiful.
10041-8{_}2105{_}I adore you, Patricia.
10041-8{_}2106{_}Would you mind if I held your hand?
10041-8{_}2107{_}It is a foolish thing to do, but it is preëminently Arcadian."
10041-8{_}2108{_}She heard him with downcast eyes; and her cheeks flushed a pink color that was agreeable to contemplation.
10041-8{_}2109{_}"Do--do you really care for me, Jack?"
10041-8{_}2110{_}she asked, softly; then cried, "No, no, you needn't answer--because, of course, you worship me madly, unboundedly, distractedly.
10041-8{_}2111{_}They all do, but you do it more convincingly.
10041-8{_}2112{_}You have been taking lessons at night-school, I dare say, at all sorts of murky institutions.
10041-8{_}2113{_}And, Jack, really, cross my heart, I always stopped the others when they talked this way.
10041-8{_}2114{_}I tried to stop you, too.
10041-8{_}2115{_}You know I did?"
10041-8{_}2116{_}She raised her lashes, a trifle uncertainly, and withdrew her hand from his, a trifle slowly.
10041-8{_}2117{_}"It is wrong--all horribly wrong.
10041-8{_}2118{_}I wonder at myself, I can't understand how in the world I can be such a fool about you.
10041-8{_}2119{_}I must not be alone with you again.
10041-8{_}2120{_}I must tell my husband--everything," she concluded, and manifestly not meaning a word of what she said.
10041-8{_}2121{_}"By all means," assented Mr. Charteris, readily.
10041-8{_}2122{_}"Let's tell my wife, too.
10041-8{_}2123{_}It will make things so very interesting."
10041-8{_}2124{_}"Rudolph would be terribly unhappy," she reflected.
10041-8{_}2125{_}"He would probably never smile again," said Mr. Charteris.
10041-8{_}2126{_}"And my wife--oh, it would upset Anne, quite frightfully!
10041-8{_}2127{_}It is our altruistic, nay, our bounden duty to save them from such misery."
10041-8{_}2128{_}"I--I don't know what to do!"
10041-8{_}2129{_}she wailed.
10041-8{_}2130{_}"The obvious course," said he, after reflection, "is to shake off the bonds of matrimony, without further delay.
10041-8{_}2131{_}So let's elope, Patricia."
10041-8{_}2132{_}Patricia, who was really unhappy, took refuge in flippancy, and laughed.
10041-8{_}2133{_}"I make it a rule," said she, "never to elope on Fridays.
10041-8{_}2134{_}Besides, now I think of it, there is, Rudolph--Ah, Rudolph doesn't care a button's worth about me, I know.
10041-8{_}2135{_}The funny part is that he doesn't know it.
10041-8{_}2136{_}He has simply assumed he is devoted to me, because all respectable people are devoted to their wives.
10041-8{_}2137{_}I can assure you, _mon ami_, he would be a veritable Othello, if there were any scandal, and would infinitely prefer the bolster to the divorce-court.
10041-8{_}2138{_}He would have us followed and torn apart by wild policemen."
10041-8{_}2139{_}Mr. Charteris meditated for a moment.
10041-8{_}2140{_}"Rudolph, as you are perfectly aware, would simply deplore the terribly lax modern notions in regard to marriage and talk to newspaper reporters about this much--" he measured it between thumb and forefinger --"concerning the beauty and chivalry of the South.
10041-8{_}2141{_}He would do nothing more.
10041-8{_}2142{_}I question if Rudolph Musgrave would ever in any circumstances be capable of decisive action."
10041-8{_}2143{_}"Ah, don't make fun of Rudolph!"
10041-8{_}2144{_}she cried, quickly.
10041-8{_}2145{_}"Rudolph can't help it if he is conscientious and in consequence rather depressing to live with.
10041-8{_}2146{_}And for all that he so often plays the jackass-fool about women, like Grandma Pendomer, he is a man, Jack--a well-meaning, clean and dunderheaded man!
10041-8{_}2147{_}You aren't; you are puny and frivolous, and you sneer too much, and you are making a fool of me, and--and that's why I like you, I suppose.
10041-8{_}2148{_}Oh, I wish I were good!
10041-8{_}2149{_}I have always tried to be good, and there doesn't seem to be a hatpin in the world that makes a halo sit comfortably.
10041-8{_}2150{_}Now, Jack, you know I've tried to be good!
10041-8{_}2151{_}I've never let you kiss me, and I've never let you hold my hand--until to-day-- and--and----"  Patricia paused, and laughed.
10041-8{_}2152{_}"But we were talking of Rudolph," she said, with a touch of weariness.
10041-8{_}2153{_}"Rudolph has all the virtues that a woman most admires until she attempts to live in the same house with them."
10041-8{_}2154{_}"I thank you," said Mr. Charteris, "for the high opinion you entertain of my moral character."
10041-8{_}2155{_}He bestowed a reproachful sigh upon her, and continued: "At any rate, Rudolph Musgrave has been an unusually lucky man--the luckiest that I know of."
10041-8{_}2156{_}Patricia had risen as if to go.
10041-8{_}2157{_}She turned her big purple eyes on him for a moment.
10041-8{_}2158{_}"You--you think so?"
10041-8{_}2159{_}she queried, hesitatingly.
10041-8{_}2160{_}Afterward she spread out her hands in a helpless gesture, and laughed for no apparent reason, and sat down again.
10041-8{_}2161{_}"Why?"
10041-8{_}2162{_}said Patricia.
10041-8{_}2163{_}It took Charteris fully an hour to point out all the reasons.
10041-8{_}2164{_}Patricia told him very frankly that she considered him to be talking nonsense, but she seemed quite willing to listen.
10041-8{_}2165{_}II   Sunset was approaching on the following afternoon when Rudolph Musgrave, fresh from Lichfield,--whither, as has been recorded, the bringing out of the July number of the _Lichfield Historical Associations Quarterly Magazine_ had called him,--came out on the front porch at Matocton.
10041-8{_}2166{_}He had arrived on the afternoon train, about an hour previously, in time to superintend little Roger's customary evening transactions with an astounding quantity of bread and milk; and, Roger abed, his father, having dressed at once for supper, found himself ready for that meal somewhat in advance of the rest of the house-party.
10041-8{_}2167{_}Indeed, only one of them was visible at this moment--a woman, who was reading on a rustic bench some distance from the house, and whose back was turned to him.
10041-8{_}2168{_}The poise of her head, however, was not unfamiliar; also, it is not everyone who has hair that is like a nimbus of thrice-polished gold.
10041-8{_}2169{_}Colonel Musgrave threw back his shoulders, and drew a deep breath.
10041-8{_}2170{_}Subsequently, with a fine air of unconcern, he inspected the view from the porch, which was, in fact, quite worthy of his attention.
10041-8{_}2171{_}Interesting things have happened at Matocton--many events that have been preserved in the local mythology, not always to the credit of the old Musgraves, and a few which have slipped into a modest niche in history.
10041-8{_}2172{_}It was, perhaps, on these that Colonel Musgrave pondered so intently.
10041-8{_}2173{_}Once the farthingaled and red-heeled gentry came in sluggish barges to Matocton, and the broad river on which the estate faces was thick with bellying sails; since the days of railroads, one approaches the mansion through the maple-grove in the rear, and enters ignominiously by the back-door.
10041-8{_}2174{_}The house stands on a considerable elevation.
10041-8{_}2175{_}The main portion, with its hipped roof and mullioned windows, is very old, but the two wings that stretch to the east and west are comparatively modern, and date back little over a century.
